The Duchess took no notice of\nthem even when they hit her; and the baby was howling so much already,\nthat it was quite impossible to say whether the blows hurt it or not.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, \u003cem\u003eplease\u003c/em\u003e mind what you’re doing!” cried Alice, jumping up and down\nin an agony of terror. “Oh, there goes his \u003cem\u003eprecious\u003c/em\u003e nose!” as an\nunusually large saucepan flew close by it, and very nearly carried it\noff.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“If everybody minded their own business,” the Duchess said in a hoarse\ngrowl, “the world would go round a deal faster than it does.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Which would \u003cem\u003enot\u003c/em\u003e be an advantage,” said Alice, who felt very glad to\nget an opportunity of showing off a little of her knowledge. “Just\nthink of what work it would make with the day and night! You see the\nearth takes twenty-four hours to turn round on its axis—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Talking of axes,” said the Duchess, “chop off her head!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice glanced rather anxiously at the cook, to see if she meant to take\nthe hint; but the cook was busily stirring the soup, and seemed not to\nbe listening, so she went on again: “Twenty-four hours, I \u003cem\u003ethink\u003c/em\u003e; or\nis it twelve? I—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, don’t bother \u003cem\u003eme\u003c/em\u003e,” said the Duchess; “I never could abide\nfigures!” And with that she began nursing her child again, singing a\nsort of lullaby to it as she did so, and giving it a violent shake at\nthe end of every line:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Speak roughly to your little boy,\nAnd beat him when he sneezes:\nHe only does it to annoy,\nBecause he knows it teases.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHORUS.\n(In which the cook and the baby joined):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Wow! wow! wow!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhile the Duchess sang the second verse of the song, she kept tossing\nthe baby violently up and down, and the poor little thing howled so,\nthat Alice could hardly hear the words:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I speak severely to my boy,\nI beat him when he sneezes;\nFor he can thoroughly enjoy\nThe pepper when he pleases!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHORUS.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Wow! wow! wow!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Here! you may nurse it a bit, if you like!” the Duchess said to Alice,\nflinging the baby at her as she spoke. “I must go and get ready to play\ncroquet with the Queen,” and she hurried out of the room. The cook\nthrew a frying-pan after her as she went out, but it just missed her.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice caught the baby with some difficulty, as it was a queer-shaped\nlittle creature, and held out its arms and legs in all directions,\n“just like a star-fish,” thought Alice. The poor little thing was\nsnorting like a steam-engine when she caught it, and kept doubling\nitself up and straightening itself out again, so that altogether, for\nthe first minute or two, it was as much as she could do to hold it.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs soon as she had made out the proper way of nursing it, (which was to\ntwist it up into a sort of knot, and then keep tight hold of its right\near and left foot, so as to prevent its undoing itself,) she carried it\nout into the open air. “If I don’t take this child away with me,”\nthought Alice, “they’re sure to kill it in a day or two: wouldn’t it be\nmurder to leave it behind?” She said the last words out loud, and the\nlittle thing grunted in reply (it had left off sneezing by this time).\n“Don’t grunt,” said Alice; “that’s not at all a proper way of\nexpressing yourself.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe baby grunted again, and Alice looked very anxiously into its face\nto see what was the matter with it. There could be no doubt that it had\na \u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e turn-up nose, much more like a snout than a real nose; also\nits eyes were getting extremely small for a baby: altogether Alice did\nnot like the look of the thing at all. “But perhaps it was only\nsobbing,” she thought, and looked into its eyes again, to see if there\nwere any tears.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eNo, there were no tears. “If you’re going to turn into a pig, my dear,”\nsaid Alice, seriously, “I’ll have nothing more to do with you. Mind\nnow!” The poor little thing sobbed again (or grunted, it was impossible\nto say which), and they went on for some while in silence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was just beginning to think to herself, “Now, what am I to do\nwith this creature when I get it home?” when it grunted again, so\nviolently, that she looked down into its face in some alarm. This time\nthere could be \u003cem\u003eno\u003c/em\u003e mistake about it: it was neither more nor less than\na pig, and she felt that it would be quite absurd for her to carry it\nfurther.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo she set the little creature down, and felt quite relieved to see it\ntrot away quietly into the wood. “If it had grown up,” she said to\nherself, “it would have made a dreadfully ugly child: but it makes\nrather a handsome pig, I think.” And she began thinking over other\nchildren she knew, who might do very well as pigs, and was just saying\nto herself, “if one only knew the right way to change them—” when she\nwas a little startled by seeing the Cheshire Cat sitting on a bough of\na tree a few yards off.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Cat only grinned when it saw Alice. It looked good-natured, she\nthought: still it had \u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e long claws and a great many teeth, so she\nfelt that it ought to be treated with respect.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Cheshire Puss,” she began, rather timidly, as she did not at all know\nwhether it would like the name: however, it only grinned a little\nwider. “Come, it’s pleased so far,” thought Alice, and she went on.\n“Would you tell me, please, which way I ought to go from here?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That depends a good deal on where you want to get to,” said the Cat.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I don’t much care where—” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then it doesn’t matter which way you go,” said the Cat.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“—so long as I get \u003cem\u003esomewhere\u003c/em\u003e,” Alice added as an explanation.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, you’re sure to do that,” said the Cat, “if you only walk long\nenough.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice felt that this could not be denied, so she tried another\nquestion. “What sort of people live about here?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“In \u003cem\u003ethat\u003c/em\u003e direction,” the Cat said, waving its right paw round, “lives\na Hatter: and in \u003cem\u003ethat\u003c/em\u003e direction,” waving the other paw, “lives a\nMarch Hare. Visit either you like: they’re both mad.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“But I don’t want to go among mad people,” Alice remarked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, you can’t help that,” said the Cat: “we’re all mad here. I’m mad.\nYou’re mad.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How do you know I’m mad?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You must be,” said the Cat, “or you wouldn’t have come here.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice didn’t think that proved it at all; however, she went on “And how\ndo you know that you’re mad?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“To begin with,” said the Cat, “a dog’s not mad. You grant that?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I suppose so,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, then,” the Cat went on, “you see, a dog growls when it’s angry,\nand wags its tail when it’s pleased. Now \u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e growl when I’m pleased,\nand wag my tail when I’m angry. Therefore I’m mad.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e call it purring, not growling,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Call it what you like,” said the Cat. “Do you play croquet with the\nQueen to-day?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I should like it very much,” said Alice, “but I haven’t been invited\nyet.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You’ll see me there,” said the Cat, and vanished.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was not much surprised at this, she was getting so used to queer\nthings happening. While she was looking at the place where it had been,\nit suddenly appeared again.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“By-the-bye, what became of the baby?” said the Cat. “I’d nearly\nforgotten to ask.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It turned into a pig,” Alice quietly said, just as if it had come back\nin a natural way.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I thought it would,” said the Cat, and vanished again.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice waited a little, half expecting to see it again, but it did not\nappear, and after a minute or two she walked on in the direction in\nwhich the March Hare was said to live. “I’ve seen hatters before,” she\nsaid to herself; “the March Hare will be much the most interesting, and\nperhaps as this is May it won’t be raving mad—at least not so mad as it\nwas in March.” As she said this, she looked up, and there was the Cat\nagain, sitting on a branch of a tree.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Did you say pig, or fig?” said the Cat.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I said pig,” replied Alice; “and I wish you wouldn’t keep appearing\nand vanishing so suddenly: you make one quite giddy.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“All right,” said the Cat; and this time it vanished quite slowly,\nbeginning with the end of the tail, and ending with the grin, which\nremained some time after the rest of it had gone.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well! I’ve often seen a cat without a grin,” thought Alice; “but a\ngrin without a cat! It’s the most curious thing I ever saw in my life!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eShe had not gone much farther before she came in sight of the house of\nthe March Hare: she thought it must be the right house, because the\nchimneys were shaped like ears and the roof was thatched with fur. It\nwas so large a house, that she did not like to go nearer till she had\nnibbled some more of the lefthand bit of mushroom, and raised herself\nto about two feet high: even then she walked up towards it rather\ntimidly, saying to herself “Suppose it should be raving mad after all!\nI almost wish I’d gone to see the Hatter instead!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHAPTER VII.\nA Mad Tea-Party\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThere was a table set out under a tree in front of the house, and the\nMarch Hare and the Hatter were having tea at it: a Dormouse was sitting\nbetween them, fast asleep, and the other two were using it as a\ncushion, resting their elbows on it, and talking over its head. “Very\nuncomfortable for the Dormouse,” thought Alice; “only, as it’s asleep,\nI suppose it doesn’t mind.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe table was a large one, but the three were all crowded together at\none corner of it: “No room! No room!” they cried out when they saw\nAlice coming. “There’s \u003cem\u003eplenty\u003c/em\u003e of room!” said Alice indignantly, and\nshe sat down in a large arm-chair at one end of the table.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Have some wine,” the March Hare said in an encouraging tone.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice looked all round the table, but there was nothing on it but tea.\n“I don’t see any wine,” she remarked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“There isn’t any,” said the March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then it wasn’t very civil of you to offer it,” said Alice angrily.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It wasn’t very civil of you to sit down without being invited,” said\nthe March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I didn’t know it was \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e table,” said Alice; “it’s laid for a great\nmany more than three.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Your hair wants cutting,” said the Hatter. He had been looking at\nAlice for some time with great curiosity, and this was his first\nspeech.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You should learn not to make personal remarks,” Alice said with some\nseverity; “it’s very rude.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Hatter opened his eyes very wide on hearing this; but all he \u003cem\u003esaid\u003c/em\u003e\nwas, “Why is a raven like a writing-desk?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Come, we shall have some fun now!” thought Alice. “I’m glad they’ve\nbegun asking riddles.—I believe I can guess that,” she added aloud.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Do you mean that you think you can find out the answer to it?” said\nthe March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Exactly so,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then you should say what you mean,” the March Hare went on.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I do,” Alice hastily replied; “at least—at least I mean what I\nsay—that’s the same thing, you know.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Not the same thing a bit!” said the Hatter. “You might just as well\nsay that ‘I see what I eat’ is the same thing as ‘I eat what I see’!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You might just as well say,” added the March Hare, “that ‘I like what\nI get’ is the same thing as ‘I get what I like’!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You might just as well say,” added the Dormouse, who seemed to be\ntalking in his sleep, “that ‘I breathe when I sleep’ is the same thing\nas ‘I sleep when I breathe’!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It \u003cem\u003eis\u003c/em\u003e the same thing with you,” said the Hatter, and here the\nconversation dropped, and the party sat silent for a minute, while\nAlice thought over all she could remember about ravens and\nwriting-desks, which wasn’t much.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Hatter was the first to break the silence. “What day of the month\nis it?” he said, turning to Alice: he had taken his watch out of his\npocket, and was looking at it uneasily, shaking it every now and then,\nand holding it to his ear.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice considered a little, and then said “The fourth.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Two days wrong!” sighed the Hatter. “I told you butter wouldn’t suit\nthe works!” he added looking angrily at the March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It was the \u003cem\u003ebest\u003c/em\u003e butter,” the March Hare meekly replied.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, but some crumbs must have got in as well,” the Hatter grumbled:\n“you shouldn’t have put it in with the bread-knife.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe March Hare took the watch and looked at it gloomily: then he dipped\nit into his cup of tea, and looked at it again: but he could think of\nnothing better to say than his first remark, “It was the \u003cem\u003ebest\u003c/em\u003e butter,\nyou know.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice had been looking over his shoulder with some curiosity. “What a\nfunny watch!” she remarked. “It tells the day of the month, and doesn’t\ntell what o’clock it is!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why should it?” muttered the Hatter. “Does \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e watch tell you what\nyear it is?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course not,” Alice replied very readily: “but that’s because it\nstays the same year for such a long time together.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Which is just the case with \u003cem\u003emine\u003c/em\u003e,” said the Hatter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice felt dreadfully puzzled. The Hatter’s remark seemed to have no\nsort of meaning in it, and yet it was certainly English. “I don’t quite\nunderstand you,” she said, as politely as she could.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“The Dormouse is asleep again,” said the Hatter, and he poured a little\nhot tea upon its nose.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Dormouse shook its head impatiently, and said, without opening its\neyes, “Of course, of course; just what I was going to remark myself.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Have you guessed the riddle yet?” the Hatter said, turning to Alice\nagain.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No, I give it up,” Alice replied: “what’s the answer?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I haven’t the slightest idea,” said the Hatter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Nor I,” said the March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice sighed wearily. “I think you might do something better with the\ntime,” she said, “than waste it in asking riddles that have no\nanswers.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“If you knew Time as well as I do,” said the Hatter, “you wouldn’t talk\nabout wasting \u003cem\u003eit\u003c/em\u003e. It’s \u003cem\u003ehim\u003c/em\u003e.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I don’t know what you mean,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course you don’t!” the Hatter said, tossing his head\ncontemptuously. “I dare say you never even spoke to Time!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Perhaps not,” Alice cautiously replied: “but I know I have to beat\ntime when I learn music.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Ah! that accounts for it,” said the Hatter. “He won’t stand beating.\nNow, if you only kept on good terms with him, he’d do almost anything\nyou liked with the clock. For instance, suppose it were nine o’clock in\nthe morning, just time to begin lessons: you’d only have to whisper a\nhint to Time, and round goes the clock in a twinkling! Half-past one,\ntime for dinner!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e(“I only wish it was,” the March Hare said to itself in a whisper.)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That would be grand, certainly,” said Alice thoughtfully: “but then—I\nshouldn’t be hungry for it, you know.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Not at first, perhaps,” said the Hatter: “but you could keep it to\nhalf-past one as long as you liked.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Is that the way \u003cem\u003eyou\u003c/em\u003e manage?” Alice asked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Hatter shook his head mournfully. “Not I!” he replied. “We\nquarrelled last March—just before \u003cem\u003ehe\u003c/em\u003e went mad, you know—” (pointing\nwith his tea spoon at the March Hare,) “—it was at the great concert\ngiven by the Queen of Hearts, and I had to sing\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e‘Twinkle, twinkle, little bat!\nHow I wonder what you’re at!’\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eYou know the song, perhaps?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’ve heard something like it,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It goes on, you know,” the Hatter continued, “in this way:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e‘Up above the world you fly,\nLike a tea-tray in the sky.\nTwinkle, twinkle—’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHere the Dormouse shook itself, and began singing in its sleep\n“\u003cem\u003eTwinkle, twinkle, twinkle, twinkle\u003c/em\u003e—” and went on so long that they\nhad to pinch it to make it stop.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, I’d hardly finished the first verse,” said the Hatter, “when the\nQueen jumped up and bawled out, ‘He’s murdering the time! Off with his\nhead!’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How dreadfully savage!” exclaimed Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And ever since that,” the Hatter went on in a mournful tone, “he won’t\ndo a thing I ask! It’s always six o’clock now.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA bright idea came into Alice’s head. “Is that the reason so many\ntea-things are put out here?” she asked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, that’s it,” said the Hatter with a sigh: “it’s always tea-time,\nand we’ve no time to wash the things between whiles.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then you keep moving round, I suppose?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Exactly so,” said the Hatter: “as the things get used up.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“But what happens when you come to the beginning again?” Alice ventured\nto ask.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Suppose we change the subject,” the March Hare interrupted, yawning.\n“I’m getting tired of this. I vote the young lady tells us a story.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’m afraid I don’t know one,” said Alice, rather alarmed at the\nproposal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then the Dormouse shall!” they both cried. “Wake up, Dormouse!” And\nthey pinched it on both sides at once.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Dormouse slowly opened his eyes. “I wasn’t asleep,” he said in a\nhoarse, feeble voice: “I heard every word you fellows were saying.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Tell us a story!” said the March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, please do!” pleaded Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And be quick about it,” added the Hatter, “or you’ll be asleep again\nbefore it’s done.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Once upon a time there were three little sisters,” the Dormouse began\nin a great hurry; “and their names were Elsie, Lacie, and Tillie; and\nthey lived at the bottom of a well—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What did they live on?” said Alice, who always took a great interest\nin questions of eating and drinking.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“They lived on treacle,” said the Dormouse, after thinking a minute or\ntwo.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“They couldn’t have done that, you know,” Alice gently remarked;\n“they’d have been ill.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“So they were,” said the Dormouse; “\u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e ill.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice tried to fancy to herself what such an extraordinary ways of\nliving would be like, but it puzzled her too much, so she went on: “But\nwhy did they live at the bottom of a well?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Take some more tea,” the March Hare said to Alice, very earnestly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’ve had nothing yet,” Alice replied in an offended tone, “so I can’t\ntake more.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You mean you can’t take \u003cem\u003eless\u003c/em\u003e,” said the Hatter: “it’s very easy to\ntake \u003cem\u003emore\u003c/em\u003e than nothing.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Nobody asked \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e opinion,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Who’s making personal remarks now?” the Hatter asked triumphantly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice did not quite know what to say to this: so she helped herself to\nsome tea and bread-and-butter, and then turned to the Dormouse, and\nrepeated her question. “Why did they live at the bottom of a well?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Dormouse again took a minute or two to think about it, and then\nsaid, “It was a treacle-well.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“There’s no such thing!” Alice was beginning very angrily, but the\nHatter and the March Hare went “Sh! sh!” and the Dormouse sulkily\nremarked, “If you can’t be civil, you’d better finish the story for\nyourself.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No, please go on!” Alice said very humbly; “I won’t interrupt again. I\ndare say there may be \u003cem\u003eone\u003c/em\u003e.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“One, indeed!” said the Dormouse indignantly. However, he consented to\ngo on. “And so these three little sisters—they were learning to draw,\nyou know—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What did they draw?” said Alice, quite forgetting her promise.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Treacle,” said the Dormouse, without considering at all this time.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I want a clean cup,” interrupted the Hatter: “let’s all move one place\non.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHe moved on as he spoke, and the Dormouse followed him: the March Hare\nmoved into the Dormouse’s place, and Alice rather unwillingly took the\nplace of the March Hare. The Hatter was the only one who got any\nadvantage from the change: and Alice was a good deal worse off than\nbefore, as the March Hare had just upset the milk-jug into his plate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice did not wish to offend the Dormouse again, so she began very\ncautiously: “But I don’t understand. Where did they draw the treacle\nfrom?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You can draw water out of a water-well,” said the Hatter; “so I should\nthink you could draw treacle out of a treacle-well—eh, stupid?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“But they were \u003cem\u003ein\u003c/em\u003e the well,” Alice said to the Dormouse, not choosing\nto notice this last remark.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course they were,” said the Dormouse; “—well in.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis answer so confused poor Alice, that she let the Dormouse go on for\nsome time without interrupting it.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“They were learning to draw,” the Dormouse went on, yawning and rubbing\nits eyes, for it was getting very sleepy; “and they drew all manner of\nthings—everything that begins with an M—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why with an M?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why not?” said the March Hare.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was silent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Dormouse had closed its eyes by this time, and was going off into a\ndoze; but, on being pinched by the Hatter, it woke up again with a\nlittle shriek, and went on: “—that begins with an M, such as\nmouse-traps, and the moon, and memory, and muchness—you know you say\nthings are “much of a muchness”—did you ever see such a thing as a\ndrawing of a muchness?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Really, now you ask me,” said Alice, very much confused, “I don’t\nthink—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then you shouldn’t talk,” said the Hatter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis piece of rudeness was more than Alice could bear: she got up in\ngreat disgust, and walked off; the Dormouse fell asleep instantly, and\nneither of the others took the least notice of her going, though she\nlooked back once or twice, half hoping that they would call after her:\nthe last time she saw them, they were trying to put the Dormouse into\nthe teapot.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“At any rate I’ll never go \u003cem\u003ethere\u003c/em\u003e again!” said Alice as she picked her\nway through the wood. “It’s the stupidest tea-party I ever was at in\nall my life!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eJust as she said this, she noticed that one of the trees had a door\nleading right into it. “That’s very curious!” she thought. “But\neverything’s curious today. I think I may as well go in at once.” And\nin she went.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOnce more she found herself in the long hall, and close to the little\nglass table. “Now, I’ll manage better this time,” she said to herself,\nand began by taking the little golden key, and unlocking the door that\nled into the garden. Then she went to work nibbling at the mushroom\n(she had kept a piece of it in her pocket) till she was about a foot\nhigh: then she walked down the little passage: and \u003cem\u003ethen\u003c/em\u003e—she found\nherself at last in the beautiful garden, among the bright flower-beds\nand the cool fountains.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHAPTER VIII.\nThe Queen’s Croquet-Ground\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA large rose-tree stood near the entrance of the garden: the roses\ngrowing on it were white, but there were three gardeners at it, busily\npainting them red. Alice thought this a very curious thing, and she\nwent nearer to watch them, and just as she came up to them she heard\none of them say, “Look out now, Five! Don’t go splashing paint over me\nlike that!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I couldn’t help it,” said Five, in a sulky tone; “Seven jogged my\nelbow.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOn which Seven looked up and said, “That’s right, Five! Always lay the\nblame on others!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eYou’d\u003c/em\u003e better not talk!” said Five. “I heard the Queen say only\nyesterday you deserved to be beheaded!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What for?” said the one who had spoken first.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s none of \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e business, Two!” said Seven.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, it \u003cem\u003eis\u003c/em\u003e his business!” said Five, “and I’ll tell him—it was for\nbringing the cook tulip-roots instead of onions.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSeven flung down his brush, and had just begun “Well, of all the unjust\nthings—” when his eye chanced to fall upon Alice, as she stood watching\nthem, and he checked himself suddenly: the others looked round also,\nand all of them bowed low.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Would you tell me,” said Alice, a little timidly, “why you are\npainting those roses?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eFive and Seven said nothing, but looked at Two. Two began in a low\nvoice, “Why the fact is, you see, Miss, this here ought to have been a\n\u003cem\u003ered\u003c/em\u003e rose-tree, and we put a white one in by mistake; and if the Queen\nwas to find it out, we should all have our heads cut off, you know. So\nyou see, Miss, we’re doing our best, afore she comes, to—” At this\nmoment Five, who had been anxiously looking across the garden, called\nout “The Queen! The Queen!” and the three gardeners instantly threw\nthemselves flat upon their faces. There was a sound of many footsteps,\nand Alice looked round, eager to see the Queen.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eFirst came ten soldiers carrying clubs; these were all shaped like the\nthree gardeners, oblong and flat, with their hands and feet at the\ncorners: next the ten courtiers; these were ornamented all over with\ndiamonds, and walked two and two, as the soldiers did. After these came\nthe royal children; there were ten of them, and the little dears came\njumping merrily along hand in hand, in couples: they were all\nornamented with hearts. Next came the guests, mostly Kings and Queens,\nand among them Alice recognised the White Rabbit: it was talking in a\nhurried nervous manner, smiling at everything that was said, and went\nby without noticing her. Then followed the Knave of Hearts, carrying\nthe King’s crown on a crimson velvet cushion; and, last of all this\ngrand procession, came THE KING AND QUEEN OF HEARTS.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was rather doubtful whether she ought not to lie down on her face\nlike the three gardeners, but she could not remember ever having heard\nof such a rule at processions; “and besides, what would be the use of a\nprocession,” thought she, “if people had all to lie down upon their\nfaces, so that they couldn’t see it?” So she stood still where she was,\nand waited.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhen the procession came opposite to Alice, they all stopped and looked\nat her, and the Queen said severely “Who is this?” She said it to the\nKnave of Hearts, who only bowed and smiled in reply.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Idiot!” said the Queen, tossing her head impatiently; and, turning to\nAlice, she went on, “What’s your name, child?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“My name is Alice, so please your Majesty,” said Alice very politely;\nbut she added, to herself, “Why, they’re only a pack of cards, after\nall. I needn’t be afraid of them!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And who are \u003cem\u003ethese?\u003c/em\u003e” said the Queen, pointing to the three gardeners\nwho were lying round the rose-tree; for, you see, as they were lying on\ntheir faces, and the pattern on their backs was the same as the rest of\nthe pack, she could not tell whether they were gardeners, or soldiers,\nor courtiers, or three of her own children.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How should \u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e know?” said Alice, surprised at her own courage. “It’s\nno business of \u003cem\u003emine\u003c/em\u003e.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Queen turned crimson with fury, and, after glaring at her for a\nmoment like a wild beast, screamed “Off with her head! Off—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Nonsense!” said Alice, very loudly and decidedly, and the Queen was\nsilent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe King laid his hand upon her arm, and timidly said “Consider, my\ndear: she is only a child!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Queen turned angrily away from him, and said to the Knave “Turn\nthem over!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Knave did so, very carefully, with one foot.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Get up!” said the Queen, in a shrill, loud voice, and the three\ngardeners instantly jumped up, and began bowing to the King, the Queen,\nthe royal children, and everybody else.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Leave off that!” screamed the Queen. “You make me giddy.” And then,\nturning to the rose-tree, she went on, “What \u003cem\u003ehave\u003c/em\u003e you been doing\nhere?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“May it please your Majesty,” said Two, in a very humble tone, going\ndown on one knee as he spoke, “we were trying—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e see!” said the Queen, who had meanwhile been examining the roses.\n“Off with their heads!” and the procession moved on, three of the\nsoldiers remaining behind to execute the unfortunate gardeners, who ran\nto Alice for protection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You shan’t be beheaded!” said Alice, and she put them into a large\nflower-pot that stood near. The three soldiers wandered about for a\nminute or two, looking for them, and then quietly marched off after the\nothers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Are their heads off?” shouted the Queen.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Their heads are gone, if it please your Majesty!” the soldiers shouted\nin reply.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s right!” shouted the Queen. “Can you play croquet?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe soldiers were silent, and looked at Alice, as the question was\nevidently meant for her.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes!” shouted Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Come on, then!” roared the Queen, and Alice joined the procession,\nwondering very much what would happen next.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s—it’s a very fine day!” said a timid voice at her side. She was\nwalking by the White Rabbit, who was peeping anxiously into her face.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Very,” said Alice: “—where’s the Duchess?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Hush! Hush!” said the Rabbit in a low, hurried tone. He looked\nanxiously over his shoulder as he spoke, and then raised himself upon\ntiptoe, put his mouth close to her ear, and whispered “She’s under\nsentence of execution.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What for?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Did you say ‘What a pity!’?” the Rabbit asked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No, I didn’t,” said Alice: “I don’t think it’s at all a pity. I said\n‘What for?’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“She boxed the Queen’s ears—” the Rabbit began. Alice gave a little\nscream of laughter. “Oh, hush!” the Rabbit whispered in a frightened\ntone. “The Queen will hear you! You see, she came rather late, and the\nQueen said—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Get to your places!” shouted the Queen in a voice of thunder, and\npeople began running about in all directions, tumbling up against each\nother; however, they got settled down in a minute or two, and the game\nbegan. Alice thought she had never seen such a curious croquet-ground\nin her life; it was all ridges and furrows; the balls were live\nhedgehogs, the mallets live flamingoes, and the soldiers had to double\nthemselves up and to stand on their hands and feet, to make the arches.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe chief difficulty Alice found at first was in managing her flamingo:\nshe succeeded in getting its body tucked away, comfortably enough,\nunder her arm, with its legs hanging down, but generally, just as she\nhad got its neck nicely straightened out, and was going to give the\nhedgehog a blow with its head, it \u003cem\u003ewould\u003c/em\u003e twist itself round and look\nup in her face, with such a puzzled expression that she could not help\nbursting out laughing: and when she had got its head down, and was\ngoing to begin again, it was very provoking to find that the hedgehog\nhad unrolled itself, and was in the act of crawling away: besides all\nthis, there was generally a ridge or furrow in the way wherever she\nwanted to send the hedgehog to, and, as the doubled-up soldiers were\nalways getting up and walking off to other parts of the ground, Alice\nsoon came to the conclusion that it was a very difficult game indeed.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe players all played at once without waiting for turns, quarrelling\nall the while, and fighting for the hedgehogs; and in a very short time\nthe Queen was in a furious passion, and went stamping about, and\nshouting “Off with his head!” or “Off with her head!” about once in a\nminute.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice began to feel very uneasy: to be sure, she had not as yet had any\ndispute with the Queen, but she knew that it might happen any minute,\n“and then,” thought she, “what would become of me? They’re dreadfully\nfond of beheading people here; the great wonder is, that there’s any\none left alive!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eShe was looking about for some way of escape, and wondering whether she\ncould get away without being seen, when she noticed a curious\nappearance in the air: it puzzled her very much at first, but, after\nwatching it a minute or two, she made it out to be a grin, and she said\nto herself “It’s the Cheshire Cat: now I shall have somebody to talk\nto.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How are you getting on?” said the Cat, as soon as there was mouth\nenough for it to speak with.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice waited till the eyes appeared, and then nodded. “It’s no use\nspeaking to it,” she thought, “till its ears have come, or at least one\nof them.” In another minute the whole head appeared, and then Alice put\ndown her flamingo, and began an account of the game, feeling very glad\nshe had someone to listen to her. The Cat seemed to think that there\nwas enough of it now in sight, and no more of it appeared.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I don’t think they play at all fairly,” Alice began, in rather a\ncomplaining tone, “and they all quarrel so dreadfully one can’t hear\noneself speak—and they don’t seem to have any rules in particular; at\nleast, if there are, nobody attends to them—and you’ve no idea how\nconfusing it is all the things being alive; for instance, there’s the\narch I’ve got to go through next walking about at the other end of the\nground—and I should have croqueted the Queen’s hedgehog just now, only\nit ran away when it saw mine coming!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How do you like the Queen?” said the Cat in a low voice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Not at all,” said Alice: “she’s so extremely—” Just then she noticed\nthat the Queen was close behind her, listening: so she went on,\n“—likely to win, that it’s hardly worth while finishing the game.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Queen smiled and passed on.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Who \u003cem\u003eare\u003c/em\u003e you talking to?” said the King, going up to Alice, and\nlooking at the Cat’s head with great curiosity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s a friend of mine—a Cheshire Cat,” said Alice: “allow me to\nintroduce it.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I don’t like the look of it at all,” said the King: “however, it may\nkiss my hand if it likes.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’d rather not,” the Cat remarked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Don’t be impertinent,” said the King, “and don’t look at me like\nthat!” He got behind Alice as he spoke.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“A cat may look at a king,” said Alice. “I’ve read that in some book,\nbut I don’t remember where.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, it must be removed,” said the King very decidedly, and he called\nthe Queen, who was passing at the moment, “My dear! I wish you would\nhave this cat removed!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Queen had only one way of settling all difficulties, great or\nsmall. “Off with his head!” she said, without even looking round.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’ll fetch the executioner myself,” said the King eagerly, and he\nhurried off.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice thought she might as well go back, and see how the game was going\non, as she heard the Queen’s voice in the distance, screaming with\npassion. She had already heard her sentence three of the players to be\nexecuted for having missed their turns, and she did not like the look\nof things at all, as the game was in such confusion that she never knew\nwhether it was her turn or not. So she went in search of her hedgehog.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe hedgehog was engaged in a fight with another hedgehog, which seemed\nto Alice an excellent opportunity for croqueting one of them with the\nother: the only difficulty was, that her flamingo was gone across to\nthe other side of the garden, where Alice could see it trying in a\nhelpless sort of way to fly up into a tree.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBy the time she had caught the flamingo and brought it back, the fight\nwas over, and both the hedgehogs were out of sight: “but it doesn’t\nmatter much,” thought Alice, “as all the arches are gone from this side\nof the ground.” So she tucked it away under her arm, that it might not\nescape again, and went back for a little more conversation with her\nfriend.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhen she got back to the Cheshire Cat, she was surprised to find quite\na large crowd collected round it: there was a dispute going on between\nthe executioner, the King, and the Queen, who were all talking at once,\nwhile all the rest were quite silent, and looked very uncomfortable.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe moment Alice appeared, she was appealed to by all three to settle\nthe question, and they repeated their arguments to her, though, as they\nall spoke at once, she found it very hard indeed to make out exactly\nwhat they said.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe executioner’s argument was, that you couldn’t cut off a head unless\nthere was a body to cut it off from: that he had never had to do such a\nthing before, and he wasn’t going to begin at \u003cem\u003ehis\u003c/em\u003e time of life.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe King’s argument was, that anything that had a head could be\nbeheaded, and that you weren’t to talk nonsense.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Queen’s argument was, that if something wasn’t done about it in\nless than no time she’d have everybody executed, all round. (It was\nthis last remark that had made the whole party look so grave and\nanxious.)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice could think of nothing else to say but “It belongs to the\nDuchess: you’d better ask \u003cem\u003eher\u003c/em\u003e about it.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“She’s in prison,” the Queen said to the executioner: “fetch her here.”\nAnd the executioner went off like an arrow.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Cat’s head began fading away the moment he was gone, and, by the\ntime he had come back with the Duchess, it had entirely disappeared; so\nthe King and the executioner ran wildly up and down looking for it,\nwhile the rest of the party went back to the game.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHAPTER IX.\nThe Mock Turtle’s Story\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You can’t think how glad I am to see you again, you dear old thing!”\nsaid the Duchess, as she tucked her arm affectionately into Alice’s,\nand they walked off together.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was very glad to find her in such a pleasant temper, and thought\nto herself that perhaps it was only the pepper that had made her so\nsavage when they met in the kitchen.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“When \u003cem\u003eI’m\u003c/em\u003e a Duchess,” she said to herself, (not in a very hopeful\ntone though), “I won’t have any pepper in my kitchen \u003cem\u003eat all\u003c/em\u003e. Soup\ndoes very well without—Maybe it’s always pepper that makes people\nhot-tempered,” she went on, very much pleased at having found out a new\nkind of rule, “and vinegar that makes them sour—and camomile that makes\nthem bitter—and—and barley-sugar and such things that make children\nsweet-tempered. I only wish people knew \u003cem\u003ethat\u003c/em\u003e: then they wouldn’t be\nso stingy about it, you know—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eShe had quite forgotten the Duchess by this time, and was a little\nstartled when she heard her voice close to her ear. “You’re thinking\nabout something, my dear, and that makes you forget to talk. I can’t\ntell you just now what the moral of that is, but I shall remember it in\na bit.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Perhaps it hasn’t one,” Alice ventured to remark.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Tut, tut, child!” said the Duchess. “Everything’s got a moral, if only\nyou can find it.” And she squeezed herself up closer to Alice’s side as\nshe spoke.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice did not much like keeping so close to her: first, because the\nDuchess was \u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e ugly; and secondly, because she was exactly the\nright height to rest her chin upon Alice’s shoulder, and it was an\nuncomfortably sharp chin. However, she did not like to be rude, so she\nbore it as well as she could.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“The game’s going on rather better now,” she said, by way of keeping up\nthe conversation a little.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“’Tis so,” said the Duchess: “and the moral of that is—‘Oh, ’tis love,\n’tis love, that makes the world go round!’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Somebody said,” Alice whispered, “that it’s done by everybody minding\ntheir own business!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Ah, well! It means much the same thing,” said the Duchess, digging her\nsharp little chin into Alice’s shoulder as she added, “and the moral of\n\u003cem\u003ethat\u003c/em\u003e is—‘Take care of the sense, and the sounds will take care of\nthemselves.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How fond she is of finding morals in things!” Alice thought to\nherself.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I dare say you’re wondering why I don’t put my arm round your waist,”\nthe Duchess said after a pause: “the reason is, that I’m doubtful about\nthe temper of your flamingo. Shall I try the experiment?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“He might bite,” Alice cautiously replied, not feeling at all anxious\nto have the experiment tried.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Very true,” said the Duchess: “flamingoes and mustard both bite. And\nthe moral of that is—‘Birds of a feather flock together.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Only mustard isn’t a bird,” Alice remarked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Right, as usual,” said the Duchess: “what a clear way you have of\nputting things!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s a mineral, I \u003cem\u003ethink\u003c/em\u003e,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course it is,” said the Duchess, who seemed ready to agree to\neverything that Alice said; “there’s a large mustard-mine near here.\nAnd the moral of that is—‘The more there is of mine, the less there is\nof yours.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, I know!” exclaimed Alice, who had not attended to this last\nremark, “it’s a vegetable. It doesn’t look like one, but it is.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I quite agree with you,” said the Duchess; “and the moral of that\nis—‘Be what you would seem to be’—or if you’d like it put more\nsimply—‘Never imagine yourself not to be otherwise than what it might\nappear to others that what you were or might have been was not\notherwise than what you had been would have appeared to them to be\notherwise.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I think I should understand that better,” Alice said very politely,\n“if I had it written down: but I can’t quite follow it as you say it.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s nothing to what I could say if I chose,” the Duchess replied,\nin a pleased tone.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Pray don’t trouble yourself to say it any longer than that,” said\nAlice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, don’t talk about trouble!” said the Duchess. “I make you a present\nof everything I’ve said as yet.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“A cheap sort of present!” thought Alice. “I’m glad they don’t give\nbirthday presents like that!” But she did not venture to say it out\nloud.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Thinking again?” the Duchess asked, with another dig of her sharp\nlittle chin.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’ve a right to think,” said Alice sharply, for she was beginning to\nfeel a little worried.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Just about as much right,” said the Duchess, “as pigs have to fly; and\nthe m—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBut here, to Alice’s great surprise, the Duchess’s voice died away,\neven in the middle of her favourite word ‘moral,’ and the arm that was\nlinked into hers began to tremble. Alice looked up, and there stood the\nQueen in front of them, with her arms folded, frowning like a\nthunderstorm.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“A fine day, your Majesty!” the Duchess began in a low, weak voice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Now, I give you fair warning,” shouted the Queen, stamping on the\nground as she spoke; “either you or your head must be off, and that in\nabout half no time! Take your choice!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Duchess took her choice, and was gone in a moment.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Let’s go on with the game,” the Queen said to Alice; and Alice was too\nmuch frightened to say a word, but slowly followed her back to the\ncroquet-ground.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe other guests had taken advantage of the Queen’s absence, and were\nresting in the shade: however, the moment they saw her, they hurried\nback to the game, the Queen merely remarking that a moment’s delay\nwould cost them their lives.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAll the time they were playing the Queen never left off quarrelling\nwith the other players, and shouting “Off with his head!” or “Off with\nher head!” Those whom she sentenced were taken into custody by the\nsoldiers, who of course had to leave off being arches to do this, so\nthat by the end of half an hour or so there were no arches left, and\nall the players, except the King, the Queen, and Alice, were in custody\nand under sentence of execution.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThen the Queen left off, quite out of breath, and said to Alice, “Have\nyou seen the Mock Turtle yet?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No,” said Alice. “I don’t even know what a Mock Turtle is.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s the thing Mock Turtle Soup is made from,” said the Queen.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I never saw one, or heard of one,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Come on, then,” said the Queen, “and he shall tell you his history,”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs they walked off together, Alice heard the King say in a low voice,\nto the company generally, “You are all pardoned.” “Come, \u003cem\u003ethat’s\u003c/em\u003e a\ngood thing!” she said to herself, for she had felt quite unhappy at the\nnumber of executions the Queen had ordered.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThey very soon came upon a Gryphon, lying fast asleep in the sun. (If\nyou don’t know what a Gryphon is, look at the picture.) “Up, lazy\nthing!” said the Queen, “and take this young lady to see the Mock\nTurtle, and to hear his history. I must go back and see after some\nexecutions I have ordered;” and she walked off, leaving Alice alone\nwith the Gryphon. Alice did not quite like the look of the creature,\nbut on the whole she thought it would be quite as safe to stay with it\nas to go after that savage Queen: so she waited.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Gryphon sat up and rubbed its eyes: then it watched the Queen till\nshe was out of sight: then it chuckled. “What fun!” said the Gryphon,\nhalf to itself, half to Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What \u003cem\u003eis\u003c/em\u003e the fun?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why, \u003cem\u003eshe\u003c/em\u003e,” said the Gryphon. “It’s all her fancy, that: they never\nexecutes nobody, you know. Come on!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Everybody says ‘come on!’ here,” thought Alice, as she went slowly\nafter it: “I never was so ordered about in all my life, never!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThey had not gone far before they saw the Mock Turtle in the distance,\nsitting sad and lonely on a little ledge of rock, and, as they came\nnearer, Alice could hear him sighing as if his heart would break. She\npitied him deeply. “What is his sorrow?” she asked the Gryphon, and the\nGryphon answered, very nearly in the same words as before, “It’s all\nhis fancy, that: he hasn’t got no sorrow, you know. Come on!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo they went up to the Mock Turtle, who looked at them with large eyes\nfull of tears, but said nothing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“This here young lady,” said the Gryphon, “she wants for to know your\nhistory, she do.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I’ll tell it her,” said the Mock Turtle in a deep, hollow tone: “sit\ndown, both of you, and don’t speak a word till I’ve finished.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo they sat down, and nobody spoke for some minutes. Alice thought to\nherself, “I don’t see how he can \u003cem\u003eever\u003c/em\u003e finish, if he doesn’t begin.”\nBut she waited patiently.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Once,” said the Mock Turtle at last, with a deep sigh, “I was a real\nTurtle.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThese words were followed by a very long silence, broken only by an\noccasional exclamation of “Hjckrrh!” from the Gryphon, and the constant\nheavy sobbing of the Mock Turtle. Alice was very nearly getting up and\nsaying, “Thank you, sir, for your interesting story,” but she could not\nhelp thinking there \u003cem\u003emust\u003c/em\u003e be more to come, so she sat still and said\nnothing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“When we were little,” the Mock Turtle went on at last, more calmly,\nthough still sobbing a little now and then, “we went to school in the\nsea. The master was an old Turtle—we used to call him Tortoise—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why did you call him Tortoise, if he wasn’t one?” Alice asked.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“We called him Tortoise because he taught us,” said the Mock Turtle\nangrily: “really you are very dull!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You ought to be ashamed of yourself for asking such a simple\nquestion,” added the Gryphon; and then they both sat silent and looked\nat poor Alice, who felt ready to sink into the earth. At last the\nGryphon said to the Mock Turtle, “Drive on, old fellow! Don’t be all\nday about it!” and he went on in these words:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, we went to school in the sea, though you mayn’t believe it—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I never said I didn’t!” interrupted Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You did,” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Hold your tongue!” added the Gryphon, before Alice could speak again.\nThe Mock Turtle went on.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“We had the best of educations—in fact, we went to school every day—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eI’ve\u003c/em\u003e been to a day-school, too,” said Alice; “you needn’t be so\nproud as all that.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“With extras?” asked the Mock Turtle a little anxiously.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes,” said Alice, “we learned French and music.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And washing?” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Certainly not!” said Alice indignantly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Ah! then yours wasn’t a really good school,” said the Mock Turtle in a\ntone of great relief. “Now at \u003cem\u003eours\u003c/em\u003e they had at the end of the bill,\n‘French, music, \u003cem\u003eand washing\u003c/em\u003e—extra.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You couldn’t have wanted it much,” said Alice; “living at the bottom\nof the sea.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I couldn’t afford to learn it.” said the Mock Turtle with a sigh. “I\nonly took the regular course.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What was that?” inquired Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Reeling and Writhing, of course, to begin with,” the Mock Turtle\nreplied; “and then the different branches of Arithmetic—Ambition,\nDistraction, Uglification, and Derision.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I never heard of ‘Uglification,’” Alice ventured to say. “What is it?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Gryphon lifted up both its paws in surprise. “What! Never heard of\nuglifying!” it exclaimed. “You know what to beautify is, I suppose?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes,” said Alice doubtfully: “it means—to—make—anything—prettier.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, then,” the Gryphon went on, “if you don’t know what to uglify\nis, you \u003cem\u003eare\u003c/em\u003e a simpleton.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice did not feel encouraged to ask any more questions about it, so\nshe turned to the Mock Turtle, and said “What else had you to learn?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, there was Mystery,” the Mock Turtle replied, counting off the\nsubjects on his flappers, “—Mystery, ancient and modern, with\nSeaography: then Drawling—the Drawling-master was an old conger-eel,\nthat used to come once a week: \u003cem\u003ehe\u003c/em\u003e taught us Drawling, Stretching, and\nFainting in Coils.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What was \u003cem\u003ethat\u003c/em\u003e like?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, I can’t show it you myself,” the Mock Turtle said: “I’m too\nstiff. And the Gryphon never learnt it.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Hadn’t time,” said the Gryphon: “I went to the Classics master,\nthough. He was an old crab, \u003cem\u003ehe\u003c/em\u003e was.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I never went to him,” the Mock Turtle said with a sigh: “he taught\nLaughing and Grief, they used to say.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“So he did, so he did,” said the Gryphon, sighing in his turn; and both\ncreatures hid their faces in their paws.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And how many hours a day did you do lessons?” said Alice, in a hurry\nto change the subject.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Ten hours the first day,” said the Mock Turtle: “nine the next, and so\non.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What a curious plan!” exclaimed Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s the reason they’re called lessons,” the Gryphon remarked:\n“because they lessen from day to day.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis was quite a new idea to Alice, and she thought it over a little\nbefore she made her next remark. “Then the eleventh day must have been\na holiday?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course it was,” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And how did you manage on the twelfth?” Alice went on eagerly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s enough about lessons,” the Gryphon interrupted in a very\ndecided tone: “tell her something about the games now.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHAPTER X.\nThe Lobster Quadrille\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Mock Turtle sighed deeply, and drew the back of one flapper across\nhis eyes. He looked at Alice, and tried to speak, but for a minute or\ntwo sobs choked his voice. “Same as if he had a bone in his throat,”\nsaid the Gryphon: and it set to work shaking him and punching him in\nthe back. At last the Mock Turtle recovered his voice, and, with tears\nrunning down his cheeks, he went on again:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You may not have lived much under the sea—” (“I haven’t,” said\nAlice)—“and perhaps you were never even introduced to a lobster—”\n(Alice began to say “I once tasted—” but checked herself hastily, and\nsaid “No, never”) “—so you can have no idea what a delightful thing a\nLobster Quadrille is!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No, indeed,” said Alice. “What sort of a dance is it?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why,” said the Gryphon, “you first form into a line along the\nsea-shore—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Two lines!” cried the Mock Turtle. “Seals, turtles, salmon, and so on;\nthen, when you’ve cleared all the jelly-fish out of the way—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eThat\u003c/em\u003e generally takes some time,” interrupted the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“—you advance twice—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Each with a lobster as a partner!” cried the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course,” the Mock Turtle said: “advance twice, set to partners—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“—change lobsters, and retire in same order,” continued the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Then, you know,” the Mock Turtle went on, “you throw the—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“The lobsters!” shouted the Gryphon, with a bound into the air.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“—as far out to sea as you can—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Swim after them!” screamed the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Turn a somersault in the sea!” cried the Mock Turtle, capering wildly\nabout.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Change lobsters again!” yelled the Gryphon at the top of its voice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Back to land again, and that’s all the first figure,” said the Mock\nTurtle, suddenly dropping his voice; and the two creatures, who had\nbeen jumping about like mad things all this time, sat down again very\nsadly and quietly, and looked at Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It must be a very pretty dance,” said Alice timidly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Would you like to see a little of it?” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Very much indeed,” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Come, let’s try the first figure!” said the Mock Turtle to the\nGryphon. “We can do without lobsters, you know. Which shall sing?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, \u003cem\u003eyou\u003c/em\u003e sing,” said the Gryphon. “I’ve forgotten the words.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo they began solemnly dancing round and round Alice, every now and\nthen treading on her toes when they passed too close, and waving their\nforepaws to mark the time, while the Mock Turtle sang this, very slowly\nand sadly:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Will you walk a little faster?” said a whiting to a snail.\n“There’s a porpoise close behind us, and he’s treading on my tail.\nSee how eagerly the lobsters and the turtles all advance!\nThey are waiting on the shingle—will you come and join the dance?\nWill you, won’t you, will you, won’t you, will you join the dance?\nWill you, won’t you, will you, won’t you, won’t you join the dance?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You can really have no notion how delightful it will be\nWhen they take us up and throw us, with the lobsters, out to sea!”\nBut the snail replied “Too far, too far!” and gave a look askance—\nSaid he thanked the whiting kindly, but he would not join the dance.\nWould not, could not, would not, could not, would not join the dance.\nWould not, could not, would not, could not, could not join the dance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What matters it how far we go?” his scaly friend replied.\n“There is another shore, you know, upon the other side.\nThe further off from England the nearer is to France—\nThen turn not pale, beloved snail, but come and join the dance.\nWill you, won’t you, will you, won’t you, will you join the dance?\nWill you, won’t you, will you, won’t you, won’t you join the dance?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Thank you, it’s a very interesting dance to watch,” said Alice,\nfeeling very glad that it was over at last: “and I do so like that\ncurious song about the whiting!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, as to the whiting,” said the Mock Turtle, “they—you’ve seen them,\nof course?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes,” said Alice, “I’ve often seen them at dinn—” she checked herself\nhastily.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I don’t know where Dinn may be,” said the Mock Turtle, “but if you’ve\nseen them so often, of course you know what they’re like.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I believe so,” Alice replied thoughtfully. “They have their tails in\ntheir mouths—and they’re all over crumbs.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“You’re wrong about the crumbs,” said the Mock Turtle: “crumbs would\nall wash off in the sea. But they \u003cem\u003ehave\u003c/em\u003e their tails in their mouths;\nand the reason is—” here the Mock Turtle yawned and shut his\neyes.—“Tell her about the reason and all that,” he said to the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“The reason is,” said the Gryphon, “that they \u003cem\u003ewould\u003c/em\u003e go with the\nlobsters to the dance. So they got thrown out to sea. So they had to\nfall a long way. So they got their tails fast in their mouths. So they\ncouldn’t get them out again. That’s all.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Thank you,” said Alice, “it’s very interesting. I never knew so much\nabout a whiting before.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I can tell you more than that, if you like,” said the Gryphon. “Do you\nknow why it’s called a whiting?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I never thought about it,” said Alice. “Why?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“\u003cem\u003eIt does the boots and shoes\u003c/em\u003e,” the Gryphon replied very solemnly.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice was thoroughly puzzled. “Does the boots and shoes!” she repeated\nin a wondering tone.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Why, what are \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e shoes done with?” said the Gryphon. “I mean, what\nmakes them so shiny?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice looked down at them, and considered a little before she gave her\nanswer. “They’re done with blacking, I believe.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Boots and shoes under the sea,” the Gryphon went on in a deep voice,\n“are done with a whiting. Now you know.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And what are they made of?” Alice asked in a tone of great curiosity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Soles and eels, of course,” the Gryphon replied rather impatiently:\n“any shrimp could have told you that.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“If I’d been the whiting,” said Alice, whose thoughts were still\nrunning on the song, “I’d have said to the porpoise, ‘Keep back,\nplease: we don’t want \u003cem\u003eyou\u003c/em\u003e with us!’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“They were obliged to have him with them,” the Mock Turtle said: “no\nwise fish would go anywhere without a porpoise.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Wouldn’t it really?” said Alice in a tone of great surprise.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Of course not,” said the Mock Turtle: “why, if a fish came to \u003cem\u003eme\u003c/em\u003e,\nand told me he was going a journey, I should say ‘With what porpoise?’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Don’t you mean ‘purpose’?” said Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I mean what I say,” the Mock Turtle replied in an offended tone. And\nthe Gryphon added “Come, let’s hear some of \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e adventures.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I could tell you my adventures—beginning from this morning,” said\nAlice a little timidly: “but it’s no use going back to yesterday,\nbecause I was a different person then.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Explain all that,” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“No, no! The adventures first,” said the Gryphon in an impatient tone:\n“explanations take such a dreadful time.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo Alice began telling them her adventures from the time when she first\nsaw the White Rabbit. She was a little nervous about it just at first,\nthe two creatures got so close to her, one on each side, and opened\ntheir eyes and mouths so \u003cem\u003every\u003c/em\u003e wide, but she gained courage as she\nwent on. Her listeners were perfectly quiet till she got to the part\nabout her repeating “\u003cem\u003eYou are old, Father William\u003c/em\u003e,” to the\nCaterpillar, and the words all coming different, and then the Mock\nTurtle drew a long breath, and said “That’s very curious.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s all about as curious as it can be,” said the Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It all came different!” the Mock Turtle repeated thoughtfully. “I\nshould like to hear her try and repeat something now. Tell her to\nbegin.” He looked at the Gryphon as if he thought it had some kind of\nauthority over Alice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Stand up and repeat ‘’\u003cem\u003eTis the voice of the sluggard\u003c/em\u003e,’” said the\nGryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“How the creatures order one about, and make one repeat lessons!”\nthought Alice; “I might as well be at school at once.” However, she got\nup, and began to repeat it, but her head was so full of the Lobster\nQuadrille, that she hardly knew what she was saying, and the words came\nvery queer indeed:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“’Tis the voice of the Lobster; I heard him declare,\n“You have baked me too brown, I must sugar my hair.”\nAs a duck with its eyelids, so he with his nose\nTrims his belt and his buttons, and turns out his toes.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e[later editions continued as follows\nWhen the sands are all dry, he is gay as a lark,\nAnd will talk in contemptuous tones of the Shark,\nBut, when the tide rises and sharks are around,\nHis voice has a timid and tremulous sound.]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“That’s different from what \u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e used to say when I was a child,” said\nthe Gryphon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Well, I never heard it before,” said the Mock Turtle; “but it sounds\nuncommon nonsense.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice said nothing; she had sat down with her face in her hands,\nwondering if anything would \u003cem\u003eever\u003c/em\u003e happen in a natural way again.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I should like to have it explained,” said the Mock Turtle.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“She can’t explain it,” said the Gryphon hastily. “Go on with the next\nverse.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“But about his toes?” the Mock Turtle persisted. “How \u003cem\u003ecould\u003c/em\u003e he turn\nthem out with his nose, you know?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“It’s the first position in dancing.” Alice said; but was dreadfully\npuzzled by the whole thing, and longed to change the subject.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Go on with the next verse,” the Gryphon repeated impatiently: “it\nbegins ‘\u003cem\u003eI passed by his garden\u003c/em\u003e.’”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice did not dare to disobey, though she felt sure it would all come\nwrong, and she went on in a trembling voice:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“I passed by his garden, and marked, with one eye,\nHow the Owl and the Panther were sharing a pie—”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e[later editions continued as follows\nThe Panther took pie-crust, and gravy, and meat,\nWhile the Owl had the dish as its share of the treat.\nWhen the pie was all finished, the Owl, as a boon,\nWas kindly permitted to pocket the spoon:\nWhile the Panther received knife and fork with a growl,\nAnd concluded the banquet—]\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What \u003cem\u003eis\u003c/em\u003e the use of repeating all that stuff,” the Mock Turtle\ninterrupted, “if you don’t explain it as you go on? It’s by far the\nmost confusing thing \u003cem\u003eI\u003c/em\u003e ever heard!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Yes, I think you’d better leave off,” said the Gryphon: and Alice was\nonly too glad to do so.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Shall we try another figure of the Lobster Quadrille?” the Gryphon\nwent on. “Or would you like the Mock Turtle to sing you a song?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Oh, a song, please, if the Mock Turtle would be so kind,” Alice\nreplied, so eagerly that the Gryphon said, in a rather offended tone,\n“Hm! No accounting for tastes! Sing her ‘\u003cem\u003eTurtle Soup\u003c/em\u003e,’ will you, old\nfellow?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe Mock Turtle sighed deeply, and began, in a voice sometimes choked\nwith sobs, to sing this:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Beautiful Soup, so rich and green,\nWaiting in a hot tureen!\nWho for such dainties would not stoop?\nSoup of the evening, beautiful Soup!\nSoup of the evening, beautiful Soup!\nBeau—ootiful Soo—oop!\nBeau—ootiful Soo—oop!\nSoo—oop of the e—e—evening,\nBeautiful, beautiful Soup!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Beautiful Soup! Who cares for fish,\nGame, or any other dish?\nWho would not give all else for two p\nennyworth only of beautiful Soup?\nPennyworth only of beautiful Soup?\nBeau—ootiful Soo—oop!\nBeau—ootiful Soo—oop!\nSoo—oop of the e—e—evening,\nBeautiful, beauti—FUL SOUP!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Chorus again!” cried the Gryphon, and the Mock Turtle had just begun\nto repeat it, when a cry of “The trial’s beginning!” was heard in the\ndistance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Come on!” cried the Gryphon, and, taking Alice by the hand, it hurried\noff, without waiting for the end of the song.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“What trial is it?” Alice panted as she ran; but the Gryphon only\nanswered “Come on!” and ran the faster, while more and more faintly\ncame, carried on the breeze that followed them, the melancholy words:—\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“Soo—oop of the e—e—evening,\nBeautiful, beautiful Soup!”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCHAPTER XI.\nWho Stole the Tarts?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe King and Queen of Hearts were seated on their throne when they\narrived, with a great crowd assembled about them—all sorts of little\nbirds and beasts, as well as the whole pack of cards: the Knave was\nstanding before them, in chains, with a soldier on each side to guard\nhim; and near the King was the White Rabbit, with a trumpet in one\nhand, and a scroll of parchment in the other. In the very middle of the\ncourt was a table, with a large dish of tarts upon it: they looked so\ngood, that it made Alice quite hungry to look at them—“I wish they’d\nget the trial done,” she thought, “and hand round the refreshments!”\nBut there seemed to be no chance of this, so she began looking at\neverything about her, to pass away the time.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlice had never been in a court of justice before, but she had read\nabout them in books, and she was quite pleased to find that she knew\nthe name of nearly everything there. “That’s the judge,” she said to\nherself, “because of his great wig.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe judge, by the way, was the King; and as he wore his crown over the\nwig, (look at the frontispiece if you want to see how he did it,) he\ndid not look at all comfortable, and it was certainly not becoming.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e“And that’s the jury-box,” thought Alice, “and those twelve creatures,”\n(she was obliged to say “creatures,” you see, because some of them were\nanimals, and some were birds,) “I suppose they are the jurors.” She\nsaid this last word two or three times over to herself, being rather\nproud of it: for she thought, and rightly too, that very few little\ngirls of her age knew the meaning of it at all.
